					<div id="mw-content-text" class="mw-body-content"><div class="mw-content-ltr mw-parser-output" lang="en" dir="ltr"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Archaeological site in Turkey</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1236090951">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}@media print{body.ns-0 .mw-parser-output .hatnote{display:none!important}}</style><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">"Çatalhüyük" redirects here. <figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Calibrated_Carbon_14_dates_for_%C3%87atalh%C3%B6y%C3%BCk_as_of_2013.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Calibrated_Carbon_14_dates_for_%C3%87atalh%C3%B6y%C3%BCk_as_of_2013.jpg/220px-Calibrated_Carbon_14_dates_for_%C3%87atalh%C3%B6y%C3%BCk_as_of_2013.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="177" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Calibrated_Carbon_14_dates_for_%C3%87atalh%C3%B6y%C3%BCk_as_of_2013.jpg/330px-Calibrated_Carbon_14_dates_for_%C3%87atalh%C3%B6y%C3%BCk_as_of_2013.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Calibrated_Carbon_14_dates_for_%C3%87atalh%C3%B6y%C3%BCk_as_of_2013.jpg/440px-Calibrated_Carbon_14_dates_for_%C3%87atalh%C3%B6y%C3%BCk_as_of_2013.jpg 2x" data-file-width="777" data-file-height="624" /></a><figcaption>Calibrated Carbon 14 dates for Çatalhöyük, as of 2013.<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></figcaption></figure>
<p><b>Çatalhöyük</b> (English: Chatalhoyuk <span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˌ/: secondary stress follows">ˌ</span><span title="/tʃ/: &#39;ch&#39; in &#39;China&#39;">tʃ</span><span title="/ɑː/: &#39;a&#39; in &#39;father&#39;">ɑː</span><span title="&#39;t&#39; in &#39;tie&#39;">t</span><span title="/ɑː/: &#39;a&#39; in &#39;father&#39;">ɑː</span><span title="&#39;l&#39; in &#39;lie&#39;">l</span><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="&#39;h&#39; in &#39;hi&#39;">h</span><span title="/uː/: &#39;oo&#39; in &#39;goose&#39;">uː</span><span title="/j/: &#39;y&#39; in &#39;yes&#39;">j</span><span title="/ʊ/: &#39;u&#39; in &#39;push&#39;">ʊ</span><span title="&#39;k&#39; in &#39;kind&#39;">k</span></span>/</a></span></span> <a href="/wiki/Help:Pronunciation_respelling_key" title="Help:Pronunciation respelling key"><i title="English pronunciation respelling">cha-tal-<span style="font-size:90%">HOO</span>-yuhk</i></a>; <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1177148991">.mw-parser-output .IPA-label-small{font-size:85%}.mw-parser-output .references .IPA-label-small,.mw-parser-output .infobox .IPA-label-small,.mw-parser-output .navbox .IPA-label-small{font-size:100%}</style><span class="IPA-label IPA-label-small">Turkish pronunciation:</span> <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="tr-Latn-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Turkish" title="Help:IPA/Turkish">&#91;tʃaˈtaɫhœjyc&#93;</a></span>; also <i>Çatal Höyük</i> and <i>Çatal Hüyük</i>; from <a href="/wiki/Turkish_language" title="Turkish language">Turkish</a> <i>çatal</i> "fork" + <i>höyük</i> "<a href="/wiki/Tumulus" title="Tumulus">tumulus</a>") is a <a href="/wiki/Tell_(archaeology)" title="Tell (archaeology)">tell</a> (a mounded accretion due to long-term human settlement) of a very large <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">Neolithic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chalcolithic" title="Chalcolithic">Chalcolithic</a> <a href="/wiki/Proto-city" title="Proto-city">proto-city</a> settlement in southern <a href="/wiki/Anatolia" title="Anatolia">Anatolia</a>, which existed from approximately 7500 BC to 5600 BC and flourished around 7000 BC.<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>2<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In July 2012, it was inscribed as a <a href="/wiki/UNESCO_World_Heritage_Site" class="mw-redirect" title="UNESCO World Heritage Site">UNESCO World Heritage Site</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>3<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Çatalhöyük overlooks the <a href="/wiki/Konya_Plain" class="mw-redirect" title="Konya Plain">Konya Plain</a>, southeast of the present-day city of <a href="/wiki/Konya" title="Konya">Konya</a> (ancient <a href="/wiki/Iconium" class="mw-redirect" title="Iconium">Iconium</a>) in <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a>, approximately 140&#160;km (87&#160;mi) from the twin-coned volcano of <a href="/wiki/Mount_Hasan" title="Mount Hasan">Mount Hasan</a>. The eastern settlement forms a mound that would have risen about 20&#160;m (66&#160;ft) above the plain at the time of the latest Neolithic occupation. There is also a smaller settlement mound to the west and a <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantine</a> settlement a few hundred meters to the east. The prehistoric mound settlements were abandoned before the <a href="/wiki/Bronze_Age" title="Bronze Age">Bronze Age</a>. A channel of the Çarşamba River once flowed between the two mounds, and the settlement was built on <a href="/wiki/Alluvium" title="Alluvium">alluvial clay</a> which may have been favorable for early agriculture. Currently, the closest river is the <a href="/wiki/Euphrates" title="Euphrates">Euphrates</a>.
</p>
<meta property="mw:PageProp/toc" />
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Archaeology">Archaeology</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=%C3%87atalh%C3%B6y%C3%BCk&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Archaeology"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-halign-left" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:MUFT_-_Catal_H%C3%B6y%C3%BCk_Modell.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/MUFT_-_Catal_H%C3%B6y%C3%BCk_Modell.jpg/200px-MUFT_-_Catal_H%C3%B6y%C3%BCk_Modell.jpg" decoding="async" width="200" height="150" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/MUFT_-_Catal_H%C3%B6y%C3%BCk_Modell.jpg/300px-MUFT_-_Catal_H%C3%B6y%C3%BCk_Modell.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/MUFT_-_Catal_H%C3%B6y%C3%BCk_Modell.jpg/400px-MUFT_-_Catal_H%C3%B6y%C3%BCk_Modell.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4000" data-file-height="3000" /></a><figcaption> Model of the neolithic settlement (7300 BC) of Catal Höyük</figcaption></figure>
<p>The site was first excavated by <a href="/wiki/James_Mellaart" title="James Mellaart">James Mellaart</a> in 1958. He later led a team which further excavated there for four seasons between 1961 and 1965.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>4<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>5<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>6<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>7<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> These excavations revealed this section of Anatolia as a centre of advanced culture in the Neolithic period.<sup id="cite_ref-gard_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gard-8"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>8<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Excavation revealed 18 successive layers of buildings signifying various stages of the settlement and eras of history. The bottom layer of buildings can be dated as early as 7100 BC while the top layer of the later West Mound is from 5600 BC.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>9<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Mellaart was banned from Turkey for his involvement in the <a href="/wiki/Dorak_affair" title="Dorak affair">Dorak affair</a>, in which he published drawings of supposedly important Bronze Age artifacts that later went missing.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>10<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> After this scandal, the site lay idle until 1993, when excavations began under the leadership of <a href="/wiki/Ian_Hodder" title="Ian Hodder">Ian Hodder</a>, then at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Cambridge" title="University of Cambridge">University of Cambridge</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>11<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>12<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>13<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>14<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>15<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The Hodder-led excavations ended in 2018. Hodder, a former student of Mellaart, chose the site as the first "real world" test of his then-controversial theory of <a href="/wiki/Post-processual_archaeology" title="Post-processual archaeology">post-processual archaeology</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>16<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The site has always had a strong research emphasis upon engagement with digital methodologies, driven by the project's experimental and reflexive methodological framework.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>17<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> According to Mickel, Hodder's Çatalhöyük Research Project (ÇRP) established itself as a site for progressive methodologies - in terms of adaptable and democratized recording, integration of computerized technologies, sampling strategies, and community involvement."<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>18<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>New excavations are being directed by <a href="/w/index.php?title=Ali_Umut_T%C3%BCrkcan&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Ali Umut Türkcan (page does not exist)">Ali Umut Türkcan</a> from <a href="/wiki/Anadolu_University" title="Anadolu University">Anadolu University</a>.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Culture">Culture</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=%C3%87atalh%C3%B6y%C3%BCk&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Culture"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Catal_H%C3%BCy%C3%BCk_EL.JPG" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Catal_H%C3%BCy%C3%BCk_EL.JPG/200px-Catal_H%C3%BCy%C3%BCk_EL.JPG" decoding="async" width="200" height="138" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Catal_H%C3%BCy%C3%BCk_EL.JPG/300px-Catal_H%C3%BCy%C3%BCk_EL.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Catal_H%C3%BCy%C3%BCk_EL.JPG/400px-Catal_H%C3%BCy%C3%BCk_EL.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1983" data-file-height="1373" /></a><figcaption>On-site restoration of a typical interior.</figcaption></figure>
<figure typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:%C3%87atalh%C3%B6y%C3%BCk_excavations_..jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/%C3%87atalh%C3%B6y%C3%BCk_excavations_..jpg/200px-%C3%87atalh%C3%B6y%C3%BCk_excavations_..jpg" decoding="async" width="200" height="136" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/%C3%87atalh%C3%B6y%C3%BCk_excavations_..jpg/300px-%C3%87atalh%C3%B6y%C3%BCk_excavations_..jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/%C3%87atalh%C3%B6y%C3%BCk_excavations_..jpg/400px-%C3%87atalh%C3%B6y%C3%BCk_excavations_..jpg 2x" data-file-width="1800" data-file-height="1221" /></a><figcaption>The earliest excavations of the site.</figcaption></figure>
<figure typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:%C3%87atalh%C3%B6y%C3%BCk_trench.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/%C3%87atalh%C3%B6y%C3%BCk_trench.jpg/200px-%C3%87atalh%C3%B6y%C3%BCk_trench.jpg" decoding="async" width="200" height="134" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/%C3%87atalh%C3%B6y%C3%BCk_trench.jpg/300px-%C3%87atalh%C3%B6y%C3%BCk_trench.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/%C3%87atalh%C3%B6y%C3%BCk_trench.jpg/400px-%C3%87atalh%C3%B6y%C3%BCk_trench.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2000" data-file-height="1339" /></a><figcaption>Deep trenches in the site.</figcaption></figure>

subtitles</figcaption></figure>
<p>Çatalhöyük was composed entirely of domestic buildings with no obvious public buildings. While some of the larger rooms have rather ornate <a href="/wiki/Murals" class="mw-redirect" title="Murals">murals</a>, the purpose of others remains unclear.<sup id="cite_ref-gard_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gard-8"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>8<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Initial estimates suggested an average population of between 5,000 and 7,000. However, more recent work using revised information on residential building distribution, and archaeological and ethnographic data estimate between 600 and 800 people in an average year lived at Çatalhöyük during the period of the earlier East Mound between 6700 and 6500 BC.<sup id="cite_ref-c841_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-c841-19"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>19<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The sites were set up as large numbers of buildings clustered together. Households looked to their neighbors for help, trade, and possible marriage for their children.<sup id="cite_ref-Maynes_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Maynes-20"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>20<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The inhabitants lived in <a href="/wiki/Mudbrick" title="Mudbrick">mudbrick</a> houses that were crammed together in an aggregate structure. No footpaths or streets were used between the dwellings, which were clustered in a honeycomb-like maze. Most were accessed by holes in the ceiling and doors on the side of the houses, with doors reached by ladders and stairs. The rooftops were effectively streets. The ceiling openings also served as the only source of ventilation, allowing smoke from the houses' open hearths and ovens to escape. 
Houses had <a href="/wiki/Plaster" title="Plaster">plaster</a> interiors accessed by squared-off timber ladders or steep stairs. These were usually on the south wall of the room, as were <a href="/wiki/Cooking_hearth" class="mw-redirect" title="Cooking hearth">cooking hearths</a> and ovens. The main rooms contained raised platforms that may have been used for a range of domestic activities. Typical houses contained two rooms for everyday activity, such as cooking and crafting.<sup id="cite_ref-Maynes_20-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Maynes-20"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>20<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> All interior walls and platforms were plastered to a smooth finish.<sup id="cite_ref-gard_8-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gard-8"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>8<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Ancillary rooms were used as storage, and were accessed through low openings from main rooms.
</p><p>All rooms were kept scrupulously clean. Archaeologists identified very little rubbish in the buildings, finding <a href="/wiki/Midden" title="Midden">middens</a> outside the ruins, with sewage and <a href="/wiki/Food_waste" class="mw-redirect" title="Food waste">food waste</a>, as well as significant amounts of ash from burning wood, reeds, and animal dung.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>21<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In good weather, many daily activities may also have taken place on the rooftops, which may have formed a plaza. In later periods, large communal ovens appear to have been built on these rooftops. Over time, houses were renewed by partial demolition and rebuilding on a foundation of rubble, which was how the mound was gradually built up. As many as eighteen levels of settlement have been uncovered.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>22<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>As a part of ritual life, the people of Çatalhöyük buried their dead within the village.<sup id="cite_ref-Maynes_20-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Maynes-20"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>20<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Human remains have been found in pits beneath the floors and especially beneath hearths, the platforms within the main rooms, and under beds. Bodies were tightly flexed before burial and were often placed in <a href="/wiki/Baskets" class="mw-redirect" title="Baskets">baskets</a> or wound and wrapped in reed mats. Disarticulated bones in some graves suggest that bodies may have been exposed in the open air for a time before the bones were gathered and buried. In some cases, graves were disturbed, and the individual's head removed from the skeleton. These heads may have been used in rituals, as some were found in other areas of the community. In a woman's grave, <a href="/wiki/Spindle_whorl" title="Spindle whorl">spinning whorls</a> were recovered and in a man's grave, <a href="/wiki/Stone_axes" class="mw-redirect" title="Stone axes">stone axes</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Maynes_20-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Maynes-20"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>20<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Some <a href="/wiki/Plastered_human_skulls" title="Plastered human skulls">skulls were plastered</a> and painted with <a href="/wiki/Ochre" title="Ochre">ochre</a> to recreate faces, a custom more characteristic of Neolithic sites in Syria and Neolithic <a href="/wiki/Jericho" title="Jericho">Jericho</a> than at sites closer by.
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-left" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Detail_of_the_mural_showing_the_hind_part_of_the_aurochs,_a_deer_and_hunters..jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Detail_of_the_mural_showing_the_hind_part_of_the_aurochs%2C_a_deer_and_hunters..jpg/220px-Detail_of_the_mural_showing_the_hind_part_of_the_aurochs%2C_a_deer_and_hunters..jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="148" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Detail_of_the_mural_showing_the_hind_part_of_the_aurochs%2C_a_deer_and_hunters..jpg/330px-Detail_of_the_mural_showing_the_hind_part_of_the_aurochs%2C_a_deer_and_hunters..jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Detail_of_the_mural_showing_the_hind_part_of_the_aurochs%2C_a_deer_and_hunters..jpg/440px-Detail_of_the_mural_showing_the_hind_part_of_the_aurochs%2C_a_deer_and_hunters..jpg 2x" data-file-width="1800" data-file-height="1214" /></a><figcaption>Detail of the mural showing the hind part of the aurochs, a deer and hunters.</figcaption></figure>
<p>Vivid <a href="/wiki/Murals" class="mw-redirect" title="Murals">murals</a> and <a href="/wiki/Figurine" title="Figurine">figurines</a> are found throughout the settlement on interior and exterior walls. Distinctive clay figurines of women, notably the <a href="/wiki/Seated_Woman_of_%C3%87atalh%C3%B6y%C3%BCk" title="Seated Woman of Çatalhöyük">Seated Woman of Çatalhöyük</a>, have been found in the upper levels of the site.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>23<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Although no identifiable temples have been found, the graves, murals, and figurines suggest that the people of Çatalhöyük had a religion rich in symbols. Rooms with concentrations of these items may have been <a href="/wiki/Shrine" title="Shrine">shrines</a> or public meeting areas. Predominant images include men with erect phalluses, hunting scenes, red images of the now extinct <a href="/wiki/Aurochs" title="Aurochs">aurochs</a> (wild cattle) and <a href="/wiki/Stags" class="mw-redirect" title="Stags">stags</a>, and vultures swooping down on headless figures.<sup id="cite_ref-gard_8-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gard-8"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>8<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Relief" title="Relief">Relief</a> figures are carved on walls, such as of lionesses facing one another.
</p><p>Heads of animals, especially of cattle, were mounted on walls. A painting of the village, with the twin mountain peaks of <a href="/wiki/Mount_Hasan" title="Mount Hasan">Hasan Dağ</a> in the background,<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>24<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> is frequently cited as the <a href="/wiki/History_of_cartography" title="History of cartography">world's oldest map</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>25<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and the first <a href="/wiki/Landscape" title="Landscape">landscape</a> painting.<sup id="cite_ref-gard_8-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gard-8"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>8<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> However, some archaeologists question this interpretation. Stephanie Meece, for example, argues that it is more likely a painting of a leopard skin instead of a volcano, and a decorative geometric design instead of a map.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>26<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Religion">Religion</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=%C3%87atalh%C3%B6y%C3%BCk&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Religion"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:AnkaraMuseumCatal.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/AnkaraMuseumCatal.jpg/220px-AnkaraMuseumCatal.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="158" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/AnkaraMuseumCatal.jpg/330px-AnkaraMuseumCatal.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/AnkaraMuseumCatal.jpg/440px-AnkaraMuseumCatal.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1633" data-file-height="1174" /></a><figcaption>Mural, <a href="/wiki/Museum_of_Anatolian_Civilizations" title="Museum of Anatolian Civilizations">Museum of Anatolian Civilizations</a>.</figcaption></figure>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Mural_from_%C3%87atalh%C3%B6y%C3%BCk_excavated_by_James_Mellaart_showing_neolithic_hunters_attacking_an_aurochs_(Bos_primigenius)..jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Mural_from_%C3%87atalh%C3%B6y%C3%BCk_excavated_by_James_Mellaart_showing_neolithic_hunters_attacking_an_aurochs_%28Bos_primigenius%29..jpg/220px-Mural_from_%C3%87atalh%C3%B6y%C3%BCk_excavated_by_James_Mellaart_showing_neolithic_hunters_attacking_an_aurochs_%28Bos_primigenius%29..jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="146" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Mural_from_%C3%87atalh%C3%B6y%C3%BCk_excavated_by_James_Mellaart_showing_neolithic_hunters_attacking_an_aurochs_%28Bos_primigenius%29..jpg/330px-Mural_from_%C3%87atalh%C3%B6y%C3%BCk_excavated_by_James_Mellaart_showing_neolithic_hunters_attacking_an_aurochs_%28Bos_primigenius%29..jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Mural_from_%C3%87atalh%C3%B6y%C3%BCk_excavated_by_James_Mellaart_showing_neolithic_hunters_attacking_an_aurochs_%28Bos_primigenius%29..jpg/440px-Mural_from_%C3%87atalh%C3%B6y%C3%BCk_excavated_by_James_Mellaart_showing_neolithic_hunters_attacking_an_aurochs_%28Bos_primigenius%29..jpg 2x" data-file-width="1798" data-file-height="1190" /></a><figcaption>Neolithic hunters attacking an aurochs, Museum of Anatolian Civilizations.</figcaption></figure>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Seated_Woman_of_%C3%87atalh%C3%B6y%C3%BCk_on_black_background.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Seated_Woman_of_%C3%87atalh%C3%B6y%C3%BCk_on_black_background.jpg/170px-Seated_Woman_of_%C3%87atalh%C3%B6y%C3%BCk_on_black_background.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="227" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Seated_Woman_of_%C3%87atalh%C3%B6y%C3%BCk_on_black_background.jpg/255px-Seated_Woman_of_%C3%87atalh%C3%B6y%C3%BCk_on_black_background.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Seated_Woman_of_%C3%87atalh%C3%B6y%C3%BCk_on_black_background.jpg/340px-Seated_Woman_of_%C3%87atalh%C3%B6y%C3%BCk_on_black_background.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2244" data-file-height="2992" /></a><figcaption><a href="/wiki/Seated_Woman_of_%C3%87atalh%C3%B6y%C3%BCk" title="Seated Woman of Çatalhöyük">Seated goddess</a> flanked by two <a href="/wiki/Felidae" title="Felidae">felines</a>, leopards or lionesses</figcaption></figure>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1236090951"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/History_of_religion" title="History of religion">History of religion</a></div>
<p>A feature of Çatalhöyük are its female figurines. Mellaart, the original excavator, argued that these carefully made figurines, carved and molded from <a href="/wiki/Marble" title="Marble">marble</a>, blue and brown <a href="/wiki/Limestone" title="Limestone">limestone</a>, <a href="/wiki/Schist" title="Schist">schist</a>, calcite, <a href="/wiki/Basalt" title="Basalt">basalt</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alabaster" title="Alabaster">alabaster</a>, and clay, represented a <a href="/wiki/Female_deity" class="mw-redirect" title="Female deity">female deity</a>. Although a male deity existed as well, "statues of a female <a href="/wiki/Deity" title="Deity">deity</a> far outnumber those of the male deity, who moreover, does not appear to be represented at all after Level&#160;VI".<sup id="cite_ref-mellart181_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mellart181-27"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>27<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> To date, eighteen levels have been identified. These figurines were found primarily in areas Mellaart believed to be shrines. The stately goddess seated on a <a href="/wiki/Throne" title="Throne">throne</a> flanked by two lionesses was found in a grain bin, which Mellaart suggests might have been a means of ensuring the harvest or protecting the food supply.<sup id="cite_ref-mellart180_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mellart180-28"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>28<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Whereas Mellaart excavated nearly two hundred buildings in four seasons, the current excavator, Ian Hodder, spent an entire season excavating one building alone.<sup id="cite_ref-balter127_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-balter127-29"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>29<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Hodder and his team, in 2004 and 2005, began to believe that the patterns suggested by Mellaart were false. They found one similar figurine, but the vast majority did not imitate the <a href="/wiki/Mother_Goddess" class="mw-redirect" title="Mother Goddess">Mother Goddess</a> style that Mellaart suggested. Instead of a Mother Goddess culture, Hodder points out that the site gives little indication of a <a href="/wiki/Matriarchy" title="Matriarchy">matriarchy</a> or <a href="/wiki/Patriarchy" title="Patriarchy">patriarchy</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>30<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1244412712">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 32px}.mw-parser-output .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;margin-top:0}@media(min-width:500px){.mw-parser-output .templatequotecite{padding-left:1.6em}}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>There are full breasts on which the hands rest, and the stomach is extended in the central part. There is a hole in the top for the head which is missing. As one turns the figurine around one notices that the arms are very thin, and then on the back of the figurine one sees a depiction of either a skeleton or the bones of a very thin and depleted human. The ribs and vertebrae are clear, as are the <a href="/wiki/Scapula" title="Scapula">scapulae</a> and the main pelvic bones. The figurine can be interpreted in a number of ways – as a woman turning into an ancestor, as a woman associated with death, or as death and life conjoined. It is possible that the lines around the body represent wrapping rather than ribs. Whatever the specific interpretation, this is a unique piece that may force us to change our views of the nature of Çatalhöyük society and imagery. Perhaps the importance of female imagery was related to some special role of the female in relation to death as much as to the roles of mother and nurturer.<sup id="cite_ref-hodder_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hodder-31"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>31<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>In an article in the <i><a href="/wiki/Turkish_Daily_News" class="mw-redirect" title="Turkish Daily News">Turkish Daily News</a></i>, Hodder is reported as denying that Çatalhöyük was a matriarchal society and quoted as saying "When we look at what they eat and drink and at their social statues, we see that men and women had the same social status. There was a balance of power. Another example is the skulls found. If one's social status was of high importance in Çatalhöyük, the body and head were separated after death. The number of female and male skulls found during the excavations is almost equal."<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>32<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In another article in the <i>Hurriyet Daily News</i> Hodder is reported to say "We have learned that men and women were equally approached".<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>33<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In a report in September 2009 on the discovery of around 2000 figurines Hodder is quoted as saying:
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1244412712"><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Çatalhöyük was excavated in the 1960s in a methodical way, but not using the full range of natural science techniques that are available to us today. Sir James Mellaart who excavated the site in the 1960s came up with all sorts of ideas about the way the site was organized and how it was lived in and so on ... We've now started working there since the mid 1990s and come up with very different ideas about the site. One of the most obvious examples of that is that Çatalhöyük is perhaps best known for the idea of the mother <a href="/wiki/Goddess" title="Goddess">goddess</a>. But our work more recently has tended to show that in fact there is very little evidence of a mother goddess and very little evidence of some sort of female-based matriarchy. That's just one of the many myths that the modern scientific work is undermining.<sup id="cite_ref-O&#39;Brien_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-O&#39;Brien-34"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>34<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>Professor <a href="/wiki/Lynn_Meskell" title="Lynn Meskell">Lynn Meskell</a> explained that while the original excavations had found only 200 figures, the new excavations had uncovered 2,000 figures, most of which depicted animals, and fewer than 5% of the figurines depicted women.<sup id="cite_ref-O&#39;Brien_34-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-O&#39;Brien-34"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>34<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Estonian folklorist <a href="/wiki/Uku_Masing" title="Uku Masing">Uku Masing</a> has suggested as early as in 1976, that Çatalhöyük was probably a hunting and gathering religion and the Mother Goddess figurine did not represent a female deity. He implied that perhaps a longer period of time was needed to develop symbols for agricultural rites.<sup id="cite_ref-Masing_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Masing-35"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>35<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> His theory was developed in the paper "Some remarks on the mythology of the people of Catal Hüyük".<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>36<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Economy">Economy</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=%C3%87atalh%C3%B6y%C3%BCk&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Economy"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Çatalhöyük has strong evidence of an <a href="/wiki/Egalitarianism" title="Egalitarianism">egalitarian</a> society, as no houses with distinctive features (belonging to <a href="/wiki/Monarch" title="Monarch">royalty</a> or <a href="/wiki/Priest" title="Priest">religious hierarchy</a> for example) have been found so far. The most recent investigations also reveal little <a href="/wiki/Social_stratification" title="Social stratification">social distinction</a> based on gender, with men and women receiving equivalent nutrition and seeming to have equal social status, as typically found in <a href="/wiki/Paleolithic" title="Paleolithic">Paleolithic</a> cultures.<sup id="cite_ref-Stavrianos_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stavrianos-37"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>37<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Gutrie_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gutrie-38"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>38<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Children observed domestic areas. They learned how to perform rituals and how to build or repair houses by watching the adults make statues, beads, and other objects.<sup id="cite_ref-Maynes_20-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Maynes-20"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>20<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
Çatalhöyük's spatial layout may be due to the close kin relations exhibited amongst the people. It can be seen, in the layout, that the people were "divided into two groups who lived on opposite sides of the town, separated by a gully." Furthermore, because no nearby towns were found from which marriage partners could be drawn, "this spatial separation must have marked two intermarrying kinship groups." This would help explain how a settlement so early on would become so large.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>39<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:%C3%87atalh%C3%B6y%C3%BCk_kaz%C4%B1_alan%C4%B1_%C3%A7at%C4%B1s%C4%B1.JPG" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/%C3%87atalh%C3%B6y%C3%BCk_kaz%C4%B1_alan%C4%B1_%C3%A7at%C4%B1s%C4%B1.JPG/220px-%C3%87atalh%C3%B6y%C3%BCk_kaz%C4%B1_alan%C4%B1_%C3%A7at%C4%B1s%C4%B1.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/%C3%87atalh%C3%B6y%C3%BCk_kaz%C4%B1_alan%C4%B1_%C3%A7at%C4%B1s%C4%B1.JPG/330px-%C3%87atalh%C3%B6y%C3%BCk_kaz%C4%B1_alan%C4%B1_%C3%A7at%C4%B1s%C4%B1.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/%C3%87atalh%C3%B6y%C3%BCk_kaz%C4%B1_alan%C4%B1_%C3%A7at%C4%B1s%C4%B1.JPG/440px-%C3%87atalh%C3%B6y%C3%BCk_kaz%C4%B1_alan%C4%B1_%C3%A7at%C4%B1s%C4%B1.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3072" data-file-height="2304" /></a><figcaption>Protective roof of the archeological site.</figcaption></figure>
<p>In the upper levels of the site, it becomes apparent that the people of Çatalhöyük were honing skills in agriculture and the domestication of animals. Female figurines have been found within bins used for storage of cereals, such as wheat and <a href="/wiki/Barley" title="Barley">barley</a>, and the figurines are presumed to be of a deity protecting the grain. <a href="/wiki/Peas" class="mw-redirect" title="Peas">Peas</a> were also grown, and <a href="/wiki/Almonds" class="mw-redirect" title="Almonds">almonds</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pistachio" title="Pistachio">pistachios</a>, and fruit were harvested from trees in the surrounding hills. <a href="/wiki/Domestic_sheep" class="mw-redirect" title="Domestic sheep">Sheep</a> were domesticated and evidence suggests the beginning of cattle <a href="/wiki/Domestication" title="Domestication">domestication</a> as well. However, <a href="/wiki/Hunting" title="Hunting">hunting</a> continued to be a major source of food for the community. <a href="/wiki/Pottery" title="Pottery">Pottery</a> and <a href="/wiki/Obsidian" title="Obsidian">obsidian</a> tools appear to have been major industries; obsidian tools were probably both used and also traded for items such as <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean" class="mw-redirect" title="Mediterranean">Mediterranean</a> sea shells and <a href="/wiki/Flint" title="Flint">flint</a> from <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">Syria</a>. Noting the lack of hierarchy and <a href="/wiki/Economic_inequality" title="Economic inequality">economic inequality</a>, historian and anti-capitalist author <a href="/wiki/Murray_Bookchin" title="Murray Bookchin">Murray Bookchin</a> has argued that Çatalhöyük was an early example of <a href="/wiki/Anarcho-communism" class="mw-redirect" title="Anarcho-communism">anarcho-communism</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>40<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Conversely, a 2014 paper argues that the picture of Çatalhöyük is more complex and that while there seemed to have been an egalitarian distribution of cooking tools and some stone tools, unbroken <a href="/wiki/Quern-stone" title="Quern-stone">quern-stones</a> and storage units were more unevenly distributed. Private property existed but shared tools also existed. It was also suggested that Çatalhöyük was becoming less egalitarian, with greater inter-generational wealth transmission.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>41<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Museum">Museum</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=%C3%87atalh%C3%B6y%C3%BCk&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Museum"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>In 2023 a new state of the art museum has opened on the site, constructed by the Konya municipality. As at October 2024 there is a bookshop and cafe added to the site. Visitors are charged 5 Euros per person for entry (non Turkish visitors). There are numerous visitor activated information desks some of which are in English as well as Turkish. Full information on all aspects of the various discoveries are available in 8 rooms including an underground reconstruction of a typical dwelling used by the occupants 90 centuries ago.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2024)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p>
